function
google_ad_request_done(google_ads)
{




window['google_ads']
=
google_ads;




if(google_ads.length
!=
0)
{








apply_ad_config(google_ads.length);









render_ads(true,
window['ad_config_top']);









if(window['backfillParam'])
{












window['backfillParam']['pl_rnum']
=
google_ads.length;












window['backfillParam']['pl_time']
=
Date.now()
-
afc_ad_start_time;








}




}
}

function
apply_ad_config(num)
{
window['ad_config_top']
=
Math.min(2,
num);
window['ad_config_bot']
=
Math.min(2,
num);
}

google_ad_client
=
'ca-aj-cat';
google_ad_channel
=
'cx_ask_wiki,ad_dirN';
google_ad_output
=
'js';
google_max_num_ads
=
'4';
google_ad_type
=
'text';
google_image_size
=
'';
google_num_ads_recieved=
'';
google_language
=
'';
google_country
=
'';
google_encoding
=
'utf8';
google_safe
=
'medium';
google_adtest
=
'off';
google_ad_section
=
'default';
google_page_url
=
'';
google_hints
=
'Depeche_Mode';
google_kw
=
'';
google_kw_type
=
'broad';


var
backfillParam
=
{




pl_rqnum
:
google_max_num_ads,




pl_hints
:
google_hints,




pl_rp
:
"false",




pl_kw
:
google_kw,




pl_cl
:
google_ad_client,




pl_ch
:
google_ad_channel,




pl_hex
:
"#FDFEEE",




pl_type
:
"afc",




pl_clksp
:
"none"};

var
word_arr
;

var
word_arr_len
;


function
highlightQuery(input_str)
{

var
queryTokenArray
=
"depeche,_,mode,";


var
tok_arr
=
queryTokenArray.split(",");

for(tok_arr_idx=0;
tok_arr_idx
<
tok_arr.length-1;
tok_arr_idx
++)
{

if(isCombinationWord(tok_arr[tok_arr_idx])){
word_arr
=
tok_arr[tok_arr_idx].split("-");
word_arr_len
=
word_arr.length;
first_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(word_arr[0],0);

if(first_pos
!=-1)
{
input_str
=
highlightCombinationWords(input_str,first_pos);
}
}
else
{
first_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(tok_arr[tok_arr_idx],0);
if(first_pos
!=
-1
&&
tok_arr[tok_arr_idx].length
>
0)
{
input_str
=
highlightSimpleWords(input_str,tok_arr[tok_arr_idx],first_pos);
}
}
}
return
input_str;
}

function
isCombinationWord(word){
if(word.indexOf("-")
!=
-1){
return
true;
}
else
{
return
false;
}
}


function
isAlpha(ch)
{
if
(ch
!=
null
&&
ch.match(/^[a-zA-Z0-9]+$/)){
return
true;
}
return
false;
}

function
isQueryinInput(firstindex,
input_str,
j){
for(i=firstindex;
i<
input_str.length
;
++i)
{
var
currindex
=
i;

if(!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(i)))
{
if(firstindex
==
currindex)

{
firstindex++;
}
else
{
if(word_arr[j]
!=
input_str.substring(firstindex,currindex).toLowerCase())
return
-1;
if
(j
==
word_arr_len-1)
return
currindex;
else
return
isQueryinInput(currindex+1,input_str,++j);
}
}
}

if(j
<
word_arr_len)
{
if(word_arr[j]
!=
input_str.substring(firstindex,currindex+1).toLowerCase())
return
-1;
}

return
(currindex
+
1)
;
}


function

highlightCombinationWords(input_str,first_pos)
{
exactmatch
=
isQueryinInput(first_pos+word_arr[0].length,input_str,1)
;
if(exactmatch
!=-1
)

{
bold_str
=
"<b>"
+
input_str.substring(first_pos,exactmatch)
+
"</b>";
input_str
=
input_str.substring(0,first_pos)
+
bold_str
+
input_str.substring(exactmatch);
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(word_arr[0],exactmatch+7);
while(next_pos
!=
-1)
{
return
highlightCombinationWords(input_str,next_pos);
}
}
return
input_str;
}


function

highlightSimpleWords(input_str,token,
first_pos)
{
var
end_pos
=
first_pos
+
token.length;
if(!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(end_pos))
&&
!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(first_pos-1))){
bold_str
=
"<b>"
+
input_str.substring(first_pos,end_pos)
+
"</b>";
input_str
=
input_str.substring(0,first_pos)
+
bold_str
+
input_str.substring(end_pos);
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(token,end_pos+7);
}
else
{
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(token,end_pos);
}

while(next_pos
!=
-1)
{
return
highlightSimpleWords(input_str,token,next_pos);
}
return
input_str;
}</script>

style="border-bottom:1px

target="_blank"
title="Help:IPA
for
English"><span
title="'d'
in

style="border-bottom:1px
dotted">d</span></a></span><span
title="Representation
in
the
International
Phonetic
Alphabet
(IPA)"
class="IPA"><a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English"
target="_blank"
title="Help:IPA
for
English">/</a></span></span>
are
an
English
<a
href="/wiki/Electronic_music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Electronic
music">electronic
music</a>
band
formed
in
1980
in
<a
href="/wiki/Basildon?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Basildon">Basildon</a>,
Essex.
The
group's
original
line-up
consisted
of
<a
href="/wiki/Dave_Gahan?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dave
Gahan">Dave
Gahan</a>
(lead
vocals,
occasional
songwriter
since
2005),
<a
href="/wiki/Martin_Gore?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Martin
Gore">Martin
Gore</a>
(keyboards,
guitar,
vocals,
chief
songwriter
after
1981),
<a
href="/wiki/Andy_Fletcher_(musician)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Andy
Fletcher
(musician)">Andy
Fletcher</a>
(keyboards),
and
<a
href="/wiki/Vince_Clarke?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Vince
Clarke">Vince
Clarke</a>
(keyboards,
chief
songwriter
1980-81).
Depeche
Mode
released
their
debut
record
in
1981,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Speak_%26_Spell_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Speak
&amp;
Spell
(album)">Speak
&amp;
Spell</a></i>,
bringing
the
band
onto
the
English
new
wave
scene.
Clarke
left
the
band
after
the
release
of
the
album,
leaving
the
band
as
a
trio
to
record
<i><a
href="/wiki/A_Broken_Frame?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="A
Broken
Frame">A
Broken
Frame</a></i>,
released
the
following
year.
<a
href="/wiki/Alan_Wilder?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Alan
Wilder">Alan
Wilder</a>
(keyboards,
drums,
occasional
songwriter)
officially
joined
the
band
afterward,
replacing
Clarke,
while
Gore
took
over
lead
songwriting
duties,
establishing
a
line
up
that
would
continue
for
the
next
thirteen
years.</p>








<p>The
band's
last
albums
of
the
1980s;
<i><a
href="/wiki/Black_Celebration?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Black
Celebration">Black
Celebration</a></i>
and
<i><a
href="/wiki/Music_for_the_Masses?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Music
for
the
Masses">Music
for
the
Masses</a></i>
established
them
as
a
dominant
force
on
the
mainstream
electronic
music
scene.
A
highlight
of
this
era
was
the
band's
concert
at
the
Pasadena
Rose
Bowl
where
they
drew
a
crowd
in
excess
of
60,000
people.
In
the
new
decade,
Depeche
Mode
released
<i><a
href="/wiki/Violator_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Violator
(album)">Violator</a></i>,
catapulting
them
to
massive
mainstream
success.
The
subsequent
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Songs_of_Faith_and_Devotion?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion">Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion</a></i>
and
the
supporting
<a
href="/wiki/Devotional_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Devotional
Tour">Devotional
Tour</a>
exacerbated
tensions
within
the
band
to
the
point
where
Alan
Wilder
quit
in
1995,
leading
to
intense
media
and
fan
speculation
that
the
band
would
split.
Now
a
trio
once
again,
the
band
released
<i><a
href="/wiki/Ultra_(Depeche_Mode_album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Ultra
(Depeche
Mode
album)">Ultra</a></i>
in
1997,
recorded
at
the
height
of
Gahan's
near-fatal
drug
abuse,
Gore's
alcoholism
and
Fletcher's
depression.
The
release
of
<i><a
href="/wiki/Exciter_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Exciter
(album)">Exciter</a></i>
confirmed
Depeche
Mode's
willingness
to
remain
together,
the
subsequent,
and
very
successful,
<a
href="/wiki/Exciter_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Exciter
Tour">Exciter
Tour</a>
being
their
first
tour
in
support
of
an
original
album
in
eight
years
since
the
<a
href="/wiki/Devotional_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Devotional
Tour">Devotional
Tour</a>
although
the
band
had
<a
href="/wiki/The_Singles_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Singles
Tour">toured
in
1998</a>
to
support
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Singles_86%E2%80%9398?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Singles
86–98">The
Singles
86&gt;98</a></i>
compilation
album.</p>








<p>Depeche
Mode
have
had
50
songs
in
the
<a
href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="UK
Singles
Chart">UK
Singles
Chart</a>
and
thirteen
top
10
albums
in
the
UK
charts,
two
of
which
debuted
at
No.
1.
According
to
<a
href="/wiki/EMI?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="EMI">EMI</a>,
Depeche
Mode
have
sold
over
100
million
albums
and
singles
worldwide,<sup
id="cite_ref-emimusic1_1-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-emimusic1-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup>
making
them
the
most
commercially
successful
electronic
band
in
music
history.<sup
id="cite_ref-billboard1_2-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-billboard1-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup>
<i><a
href="/wiki/Q_(magazine)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Q
(magazine)"
class="mw-redirect">Q</a></i>
magazine
calls
Depeche
Mode
&quot;The
most
popular
electronic
band
the
world
has
ever
known&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-Q_.28magazine.29_3-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Q_.28magazine.29-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
included
the
band
in
the
list
of
the
&quot;50
Bands
That
Changed
the
World!&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

style="overflow:hidden;height:;">

<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="History">History</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1"
title="Edit
section:
History">edit</a>]</span></h2>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Formation_and_debut_album_.281977.E2.80.931981.29">Formation
and
debut
album
(1977–1981)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2"
title="Edit
section:
Formation
and
debut
album
(1977–1981)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>Depeche
Mode's
origins
date
to
1977,
when
schoolmates
Vince
Clarke
and
Andy
Fletcher
formed
a
<a
href="/wiki/The_Cure?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Cure">The
Cure</a>-influenced<sup
id="cite_ref-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
band
called
No
Romance
In
China,
with
Clarke
on
vocals
and
guitar
and
Fletcher
on
bass.
Fletcher
would
later
recall,
&quot;Why
am
I
in
the
band?
It
was
accidental
right
from
the
beginning.
I
was
actually
forced
to
be
in
the
band.
I
played
the
guitar
and
I
had
a
bass;
it
was
a
question
of
them
roping
me
in.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-P93_6-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-P93-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
1979,
Clarke
played
guitar
in
an
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Ultravox?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Ultravox">Ultravox</a>
rip-off
band&quot;,
The
Plan,
with
friends
<a
href="/wiki/Robert_Marlow?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Robert
Marlow">Robert
Marlow</a>
and
Paul
Langwith.<sup
id="cite_ref-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
1978–79,
<a
href="/wiki/Martin_Gore?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Martin
Gore">Martin
Gore</a>
played
guitar
in
an
acoustic
duo,
Norman
and
The
Worms,
with
school
friend
Phil
Burdett
on
vocals.<sup
id="cite_ref-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
1979,
Marlow,
Gore,
and
friend
Paul
Redmond
formed
a
band
called
The
French
Look,
with
Marlow
on
vocals/keyboards,
Gore
on
guitar
and
Redmond
on
keyboards.
In
March
1980,
Clarke,
Gore
and
Fletcher
formed
a
band
called
Composition
of
Sound,
with
Clarke
on
vocals/guitar,
Gore
on
keyboards,
and
Fletcher
on
bass.</p>








<p>Soon
after
the
formation
of
Composition
of
Sound,
Clarke
heard
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Electricity_(OMD_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Electricity
(OMD
song)">Electricity</a>&quot;,
the
debut
single
by
<a
href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Borough_of_Wirral?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Metropolitan
Borough
of
Wirral">Wirral</a>
electronic
duo
<a
href="/wiki/Orchestral_Manoeuvres_in_the_Dark?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Orchestral
Manoeuvres
in
the
Dark">Orchestral
Manoeuvres
in
the
Dark</a>
(OMD).
He
said
of
the
song:
&quot;It
sounded
so
different
from
anything
I'd
heard;
that
really
made
me
want
to
make
electronic
music,
'cause
it
was
so
unique.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup>
Along
with
OMD,
other
early
influences
included
<a
href="/wiki/The_Human_League?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Human
League">The
Human
League</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Daniel_Miller_(music_producer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Daniel
Miller
(music
producer)">Daniel
Miller</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Fad_Gadget?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Fad
Gadget">Fad
Gadget</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup>
Clarke
and
Fletcher
switched
to
synthesisers,
working
odd
jobs
in
order
to
buy
or
borrow
the
instruments
from
friends.
<a
href="/wiki/Dave_Gahan?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dave
Gahan">Dave
Gahan</a>
joined
the
band
in
1980
after
Clarke
heard
him
perform
at
a
local
scout
hut
<a
href="/wiki/Jam_session?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jam
session">jam
session</a>,
singing
a
rendition
of
<a
href="/wiki/David_Bowie?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="David
Bowie">David
Bowie</a>'s
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Heroes_(David_Bowie_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Heroes
(David
Bowie
song)"
class="mw-redirect">Heroes</a>&quot;,<sup
id="cite_ref-D93_11-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-D93-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
Depeche
Mode
were
born.
When
explaining
the
choice
for
the
new
name
taken
from
a
French
fashion
magazine,
<i>D&eacute;p&ecirc;che
mode</i>
(from
French
<i>d&eacute;p&ecirc;che</i>
that
means
here
&quot;dispatch&quot;
(from
<a
href="/wiki/Old_French?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Old
French">Old
French</a>
<i>despesche/despeche</i>)
or
&quot;news
report&quot;,
and
<i>mode</i>
that
means
&quot;fashion&quot;),
Gore
said,
&quot;It
means
hurried
fashion
or
fashion
dispatch.
I
like
the
sound
of
that.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-12"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup>
But,
in
French,
the
real
and
only
meaning
of
the
magazine's
name
(and
hence
the
band's)
is
&quot;Fashion
News&quot;
or
&quot;Fashion
Update&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-13"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup>
Gore
recollects
that
the
first
time
the
band
played
as
Depeche
Mode
was
a
school
gig
in
May
1980.<sup
id="cite_ref-14"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
band
made
their
recording
debut
in
1980
on
the
<i><a
href="/wiki/Some_Bizzare_Album?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Some
Bizzare
Album">Some
Bizzare
Album</a></i>
with
the
song
&quot;Photographic&quot;,
which
was
later
re-recorded
for
their
debut
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Speak_%26_Spell_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Speak
&amp;
Spell
(album)">Speak
&amp;
Spell</a></i>.</p>








<p>The
band
made
a
<a
href="/wiki/Demo_tape?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Demo
tape"
class="mw-redirect">demo
tape</a>
but,
instead
of
mailing
the
tape
to
record
companies,
they
would
go
in
and
personally
deliver
it.
They
would
demand
the
companies
play
it;
according
to
Dave
Gahan,
&quot;most
of
them
would
tell
us
to
fuck
off.
They'd
say
'leave
the
tape
with
us'
and
we'd
say
'it's
our
only
one'.
Then
we'd
say
goodbye
and
go
somewhere
else.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-record.2Fref_15-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-record.2Fref-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>According
to
Gahan,
prior
to
securing
their
record
contract,
they
were
receiving
offers
from
all
the
major
labels.
<a
href="/wiki/Phonogram_Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Phonogram
Records">Phonogram</a>
offered
them
&quot;money
you
could
never
have
imagined
and
all
sorts
of
crazy
things
like
clothes
allowances&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-record.2Fref_15-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-record.2Fref-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>While
playing
a
live
gig
at
the
Bridge
House
in
<a
href="/wiki/Canning_Town?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Canning
Town">Canning
Town</a>,<sup
id="cite_ref-16"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup>
the
band
were
approached
by
<a
href="/wiki/Daniel_Miller_(music_producer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Daniel
Miller
(music
producer)">Daniel
Miller</a>,
an
<a
href="/wiki/Electronic_musician?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Electronic
musician"
class="mw-redirect">electronic
musician</a>
and
founder
of
<a
href="/wiki/Mute_Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Mute
Records">Mute
Records</a>,
who
was
interested
in
their
recording
a
single
for
his
burgeoning
label.<sup
id="cite_ref-17"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
result
of
this
verbal
contract
was
their
first
single,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dreaming_of_Me?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dreaming
of
Me">Dreaming
of
Me</a>&quot;,
recorded
in
December
1980
and
released
in
February
1981.
It
reached
number
57
in
the
UK
charts.
Encouraged
by
this,
the
band
recorded
their
second
single,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/New_Life_(song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="New
Life
(song)">New
Life</a>&quot;,
which
climbed
to
number
11
in
the
UK
charts
and
got
them
to
appear
on
<i><a
href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Pops?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Top
of
the
Pops">Top
of
the
Pops</a></i>.
The
band
actually
went
to
London
by
train,
dragging
their
synthesisers
all
the
way
to
the
<a
href="/wiki/BBC?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="BBC">BBC</a>'s
studios.</p>








<p>The
band's
next
single
was
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Just_Can%27t_Get_Enough_(Depeche_Mode_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Just
Can't
Get
Enough
(Depeche
Mode
song)">Just
Can't
Get
Enough</a>&quot;.
This
relentlessly
upbeat
piece
of
synthpop
became
the
band's
first
UK
top
ten
hit
and
it
remains
one
of
their
best
known
songs.
It
was
also
the
first
Depeche
Mode
song
to
get
a
music
video
and
is
the
only
one
of
the
band's
videos
to
feature
Vince
Clarke.
Depeche
Mode's
debut
album,
<i>Speak
&amp;
Spell</i>,
was
released
in
November
1981
and
peaked
at
number
ten
on
the
UK
album
charts.
Critical
reviews
were
mixed
–
<i><a
href="/wiki/Melody_Maker?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Melody
Maker">Melody
Maker</a></i>
described
it
as
a
&quot;great
album...
one
they
had
to
make
to
conquer
fresh
audiences
and
please
the
fans
who
just
can't
get
enough&quot;,<sup
id="cite_ref-18"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup>
while
<i><a
href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rolling
Stone">Rolling
Stone</a></i>
was
more
critical,
calling
the
album
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Rated_PG?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rated
PG"
class="mw-redirect">PG-rated</a>
fluff&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-19"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Clarke_departs.2C_Wilder_joins_.281981.E2.80.931982.29">Clarke
departs,
Wilder
joins
(1981–1982)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3"
title="Edit
section:
Clarke
departs,
Wilder
joins
(1981–1982)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>During
the
touring
and
promotion
for
<i>Speak
&amp;
Spell</i>,
Clarke
privately
began
to
voice
his
discomfort
at
the
direction
the
band
were
taking.
He
later
expressed
his
dissatisfaction,
saying
&quot;there
was
never
enough
time
to
do
anything.
Not
with
all
the
interviews
and
photo
sessions.&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-20"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
November
1981
Clarke
publicly
announced
that
he
was
leaving
Depeche
Mode.<sup
id="cite_ref-21"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup>
It
also
was
claimed
Clarke
was
sick
of
touring,
which
Gahan
said
years
later
was
&quot;bullshit
to
be
quite
honest&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-record.2Fref_15-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-record.2Fref-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup>
Gahan
went
on
to
say
he
&quot;suddenly
lost
interest
in
it
and
he
started
getting
letters
from
fans
asking
what
kind
of
socks
he
wore&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-record.2Fref_15-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-record.2Fref-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Soon
afterwards,
Clarke
joined
up
with
blues
singer
<a
href="/wiki/Alison_Moyet?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Alison
Moyet">Alison
Moyet</a>
to
form
<a
href="/wiki/Yazoo_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Yazoo
(band)">Yazoo</a>
(Yaz
in
the
US)
and
later,
the
duo
<a
href="/wiki/Erasure?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Erasure">Erasure</a>
with
<a
href="/wiki/Andy_Bell_(singer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Andy
Bell
(singer)">Andy
Bell</a>.
Initial
talk
of
Clarke's
continuing
to
write
material
for
the
group
ultimately
amounted
to
nothing.
According
to
third-party
sources,
Clarke
offered
the
remaining
members
of
Depeche
Mode
the
track
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Only_You_(Yazoo_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Only
You
(Yazoo
song)">Only
You</a>&quot;,
but
they
declined.<sup
id="cite_ref-22"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup>
Clarke,
however,
denied
in
an
interview
that
such
an
offer
ever
took
place
saying,
&quot;I
don't
know
where
that
came
from.
That's
not
true.&quot;
<sup
id="cite_ref-23"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
song
went
on
to
become
a
UK
Top
3
hit
for
Yazoo.
Gore,
who
had
written
&quot;Tora!
Tora!
Tora!&quot;
and
the
instrumental
&quot;Big
Muff&quot;
for
<i>Speak
&amp;
Spell</i>,
was
forced
to
become
the
band's
new
songwriter.<sup
id="cite_ref-24"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
late
1981,
the
band
placed
an
anonymous
ad
in
<i>Melody
Maker</i>
looking
for
another
musician;
it
said
&quot;Name
band,
synthesise,
must
be
under
twenty-one.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-D93_11-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-D93-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup>
Alan
Wilder,
a
keyboardist
from
West
London,
responded
and,
after
two
auditions
and
despite
being
22
years
old,
he
was
hired
in
early
1982,
initially
on
a
trial
basis
as
a
touring
member.<sup
id="cite_ref-25"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup>
Wilder
would
later
be
called
the
&quot;Musical
Director&quot;
of
the
band,
responsible
for
the
band's
sound
until
his
departure
in
1995.<sup
id="cite_ref-P93_6-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-P93-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>
As
producer
Flood
would
later
say,
&quot;[Alan]
is
sort
of
the
craftsman,
Martin's
the
idea
man
and
[Dave]
is
the
attitude.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-P93_6-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-P93-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
January
1982,
the
band
released
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/See_You_(song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="See
You
(song)">See
You</a>&quot;,
their
first
single
without
Clarke,
which
managed
to
beat
all
three
Clarke-penned
singles
in
the
UK
charts,
reaching
number
six.<sup
id="cite_ref-26"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
tour
that
followed
the
release
of
the
single
saw
the
band
playing
their
first
shows
in
North
America.
Two
more
singles,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/The_Meaning_of_Love?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Meaning
of
Love">The
Meaning
of
Love</a>&quot;,
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Leave_in_Silence?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Leave
in
Silence">Leave
in
Silence</a>&quot;,
were
released
ahead
of
the
band's
second
studio
album.
Depeche
Mode
began
work
on
their
second
album
in
July
1982.
Daniel
Miller
informed
Wilder
that
he
was
not
needed
for
the
recording
of
the
album,
as
the
band
wanted
to
prove
that
they
could
succeed
without
Vince
Clarke.<sup
id="cite_ref-27"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup>
<i><a
href="/wiki/A_Broken_Frame?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="A
Broken
Frame">A
Broken
Frame</a></i>
was
released
that
September
and
the
following
month
the
band
set
off
on
their
second
<a
href="/wiki/A_Broken_Frame_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="A
Broken
Frame
Tour"
class="mw-redirect">tour</a>
of
1982.
A
non-album
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Get_the_Balance_Right!?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Get
the
Balance
Right!">Get
the
Balance
Right!</a>&quot;
was
released
in
January
1983,
and
was
the
first
Depeche
Mode
track
to
be
recorded
with
Wilder.<sup
id="cite_ref-28"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Construction_Time_Again_.281983.29"><i>Construction
Time
Again</i>
(1983)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4"
title="Edit
section:
Construction
Time
Again
(1983)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>For
their
third
LP
<i><a
href="/wiki/Construction_Time_Again?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Construction
Time
Again">Construction
Time
Again</a></i>,
Depeche
Mode
worked
with
producer
<a
href="/wiki/Gareth_Jones_(music_producer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gareth
Jones
(music
producer)">Gareth
Jones</a>,
at
<a
href="/wiki/John_Foxx?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="John
Foxx">John
Foxx</a>'s
Garden
Studios
and
at
<a
href="/wiki/Hansa_Tonstudio?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hansa
Tonstudio">Hansa
Studios</a>
in
West
Berlin
(where
much
of
<a
href="/wiki/David_Bowie?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="David
Bowie">David
Bowie</a>'s
<a
href="/wiki/Berlin_Trilogy?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Berlin
Trilogy"
class="mw-redirect">trilogy</a>
of
seminal
electronic
albums
featuring
<a
href="/wiki/Brian_Eno?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brian
Eno">Brian
Eno</a>
had
been
produced).
The
album
saw
a
dramatic
shift
in
the
group's
sound,
due
in
part
to
Wilder's
introduction
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Synclavier?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Synclavier">Synclavier</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/E-mu_Emulator?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="E-mu
Emulator">E-mu
Emulator</a>
<a
href="/wiki/Sampler_(musical_instrument)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Sampler
(musical
instrument)">samplers</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-29"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup>
By
sampling
the
noises
of
everyday
objects,
the
band
created
an
eclectic,
<a
href="/wiki/Industrial_music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Industrial
music">industrial</a>-influenced
sound,
with
similarities
to
groups
such
as
the
<a
href="/wiki/Art_of_Noise?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Art
of
Noise">Art
of
Noise</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Einst%C3%BCrzende_Neubauten?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Einst&uuml;rzende
Neubauten">Einst&uuml;rzende
Neubauten</a>,
the
latter
later
having
work
released
on
the
Mute
label.<sup
id="cite_ref-30"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Along
with
the
music,
Gore's
songwriting
was
also
rapidly
evolving,
focusing
increasingly
on
political
and
social
issues.
A
good
example
of
the
new
sound
was
on
the
first
single
from
the
album
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Everything_Counts?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Everything
Counts">Everything
Counts</a>&quot;,
a
commentary
on
the
perceived
greed
of
multinational
corporations.<sup
id="cite_ref-31"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
a
retrospective
review
of
the
single,
Allmusic
journalist
<a
href="/wiki/Ned_Raggett?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Ned
Raggett">Ned
Raggett</a>
wrote
that
the
song
marked
a
change
in
the
band
&quot;with
Martin
Gore's
songwriting
abilities
matched
with
an
increasing
ambition
of
the
band
as
a
whole.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-32"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>&quot;Everything
Counts&quot;
got
to
number
six
in
the
UK,
also
reaching
the
Top
30
in
Ireland,
South
Africa,
Switzerland,
Sweden
and
West
Germany.
Wilder
also
contributed
two
songs
to
the
album,
&quot;The
Landscape
Is
Changing&quot;
and
&quot;Two
Minute
Warning&quot;.</p>








<p>In
September
1983,
to
promote
<i>Construction
Time
Again</i>
the
band
launched
<a
href="/wiki/Construction_Time_Again_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Construction
Time
Again
Tour">Construction
Time
Again
Tour</a>,
a
concert
tour
all
over
Europe.</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Some_Great_Reward_.281984.29_and_international_success"><i>Some
Great
Reward</i>
(1984)
and
international
success</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5"
title="Edit
section:
Some
Great
Reward
(1984)
and
international
success">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>In
their
early
years,
Depeche
Mode
had
only
really
attained
success
in
Europe
and
Australia,
however
this
changed
in
March
1984
when
they
released
the
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/People_Are_People?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="People
Are
People">People
Are
People</a>&quot;.
The
song
reached
No.
2
in
Ireland,
No.
4
in
UK
and
Switzerland
and
No.
1
in
West
Germany
-
the
first
time
a
DM
single
topped
a
country's
singles
chart
-
where
it
was
used
as
the
theme
to
West
German
TV's
coverage
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="1984
Summer
Olympics">1984
Olympics</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-33"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup>
It
belatedly
reached
No.
13
on
the
US
charts
in
mid-1985.
The
song
has
since
become
an
anthem
for
the
LGBT
community
and
was
regularly
played
at
gay
establishments
and
<a
href="/wiki/Gay_pride?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gay
pride">gay
pride</a>
festivals
in
the
late
1980s.<sup
id="cite_ref-34"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup>
<a
href="/wiki/Sire_Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Sire
Records">Sire</a>,
the
band's
North
American
record
label,
released
a
compilation
of
<a
href="/wiki/People_Are_People_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="People
Are
People
(album)">the
same
name</a>
which
included
tracks
from
<i>A
Broken
Frame</i>
and
<i><a
href="/wiki/Construction_Time_Again?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Construction
Time
Again">Construction
Time
Again</a></i>
as
well
as
several
b-sides.</p>








<p>It
was
the
tour
in
America
that
year
where
the
band
noticed
things
were
changing.
They
were,
according
to
Gore,
&quot;shocked
by
the
way
the
fans
were
turning
up
in
droves
at
the
concerts&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-record.2Fref_15-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-record.2Fref-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup>
He
said
that
although
the
concerts
were
selling
well,
they
were
still
struggling
to
sell
records.<sup
id="cite_ref-record.2Fref_15-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-record.2Fref-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
September
1984,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Some_Great_Reward?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Some
Great
Reward">Some
Great
Reward</a></i>
was
released.
<i>Melody
Maker</i>
claimed
that
the
album
made
one
&quot;sit
up
and
take
notice
of
what
is
happening
here,
right
under
your
nose.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-35"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
contrast
to
the
political
and
environmental
subjects
addressed
on
the
previous
album,
the
songs
on
<i>Some
Great
Reward</i>
were
mostly
concerned
with
more
personal
themes
such
as
sexual
politics
(&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Master_and_Servant?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Master
and
Servant">Master
and
Servant</a>&quot;),
adulterous
relationships
(&quot;Lie
to
Me&quot;),
and
arbitrary
divine
justice
(&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Blasphemous_Rumours?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Blasphemous
Rumours"
class="mw-redirect">Blasphemous
Rumours</a>&quot;).
Also
included
was
the
first
Martin
Gore
ballad
(&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Somebody_(Depeche_Mode_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Somebody
(Depeche
Mode
song)"
class="mw-redirect">Somebody</a>&quot;)
–
such
songs
would
become
a
feature
of
all
following
albums.
&quot;Somebody&quot;
was
released
as
a
double
a-side
with
&quot;Blasphemous
Rumours&quot;
and
was
the
first
single
with
Gore
on
lead
vocals.
<i>Some
Great
Reward</i>
was
the
first
Depeche
Mode
album
to
enter
the
US
album
charts,
and
it
made
the
Top
10
in
several
European
countries.</p>








<p><i><a
href="/wiki/The_World_We_Live_In_and_Live_in_Hamburg?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
World
We
Live
In
and
Live
in
Hamburg">The
World
We
Live
In
and
Live
in
Hamburg</a></i>
was
the
band's
first
video
release.
It
is
an
almost
complete
film
of
a
concert
from
their
1984
<a
href="/wiki/Some_Great_Reward_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Some
Great
Reward
Tour">Some
Great
Reward
Tour</a>,
in
<a
href="/wiki/Hamburg?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>,
Germany.
In
July
1985,
the
band
played
their
first-ever
concerts
behind
the
<a
href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Iron
Curtain">Iron
Curtain</a>,
in
<a
href="/wiki/Budapest?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Budapest">Budapest</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Warsaw?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-36"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
October
1985,
<a
href="/wiki/Mute_Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Mute
Records">Mute
Records</a>
released
a
compilation,
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Singles_81%E2%80%9385?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Singles
81–85"
class="mw-redirect">The
Singles
81&gt;85</a></i>
(<i><a
href="/wiki/Catching_Up_with_Depeche_Mode?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Catching
Up
with
Depeche
Mode">Catching
Up
with
Depeche
Mode</a></i>
in
the
US),
which
included
the
two
non-album
hit-singles
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Shake_the_Disease?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Shake
the
Disease">Shake
the
Disease</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/It%27s_Called_a_Heart?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="It's
Called
a
Heart">It's
Called
a
Heart</a>&quot;.</p>








<p>During
this
period,
in
some
circles,
the
band
became
associated
with
the
<a
href="/wiki/Gothic_subculture?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gothic
subculture"
class="mw-redirect">gothic
subculture</a>,
which
had
begun
in
Britain
in
the
early-1980s,
and
was
now
slowly
gaining
popularity
in
the
United
States.
There,
the
band's
music
had
first
gained
prominence
on
college
radio
and
<a
href="/wiki/Modern_rock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Modern
rock">modern
rock</a>
stations
such
as
<a
href="/wiki/KROQ-FM?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="KROQ-FM">KROQ</a>
in
Los
Angeles,
<a
href="/wiki/KQAK?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="KQAK">KQAK</a>
(&quot;The
Quake&quot;)
in
San
Francisco,
<a
href="/wiki/WFNX?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="WFNX">WFNX</a>
in
Boston
and
<a
href="/wiki/WLIR?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="WLIR">WLIR</a>
on
<a
href="/wiki/Long_Island,_New_York?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Long
Island,
New
York"
class="mw-redirect">Long
Island,
New
York</a>,
and
hence
they
appealed
primarily
to
an
<a
href="/wiki/Alternative_rock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Alternative
rock">alternative</a>
audience
who
were
disenfranchised
with
the
predominance
of
&quot;soft
rock
and
'disco
hell'&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-37"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup>
on
the
radio.
This
view
of
the
band
was
in
sharp
contrast
to
how
the
band
was
perceived
in
Europe,
despite
the
increasingly
dark
and
serious
tone
in
their
songs.<sup
id="cite_ref-38"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
Germany,
France
and
other
European
countries,
Depeche
Mode
were
considered
teen
idols
and
were
regularly
featured
in
European
teen
magazines.</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Black_Celebration_.281986.29"><i>Black
Celebration</i>
(1986)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6"
title="Edit
section:
Black
Celebration
(1986)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>Depeche
Mode's
musical
style
shifted
slightly
again
in
1986
with
the
release
of
their
fifteenth
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Stripped_(song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Stripped
(song)">Stripped</a>&quot;,
and
its
accompanying
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Black_Celebration?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Black
Celebration">Black
Celebration</a></i>.
Retaining
their
often
imaginative
sampling
and
beginning
to
move
away
from
the
&quot;industrial-pop&quot;
sound
that
had
characterised
their
previous
two
LPs,
the
band
introduced
an
ominous,
highly
atmospheric
and
textured
sound.
Gore's
lyrics
also
took
on
a
darker
tone
and
became
even
more
pessimistic.</p>








<p>The
music
video
for
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/A_Question_of_Time?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="A
Question
of
Time">A
Question
of
Time</a>&quot;
was
the
first
to
be
directed
by
<a
href="/wiki/Anton_Corbijn?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Anton
Corbijn">Anton
Corbijn</a>,<sup
id="cite_ref-39"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup>
beginning
a
working
relationship
that
continues
to
the
present
day.
Corbijn
has
directed
a
further
19
of
the
band's
videos
(the
latest
being
2006's
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Suffer_Well?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Suffer
Well">Suffer
Well</a>&quot;).
He
has
also
filmed
some
of
their
live
performances
and
designed
stage
sets
and
album
and
single
covers.</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Music_for_the_Masses_and_101_.281987.E2.80.931988.29"><i>Music
for
the
Masses</i>
and
<i>101</i>
(1987–1988)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7"
title="Edit
section:
Music
for
the
Masses
and
101
(1987–1988)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>1987's
<i><a
href="/wiki/Music_for_the_Masses?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Music
for
the
Masses">Music
for
the
Masses</a></i>
saw
further
alterations
in
the
band's
sound
and
working
methods.
For
the
first
time
a
producer
not
related
to
Mute,
Dave
Bascombe,
was
called
to
assist
with
the
recording
sessions
(although,
according
to
Alan
Wilder,
his
role
ended
up
being
more
that
of
an
engineer).<sup
id="cite_ref-shunteditorials1_40-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shunteditorials1-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
making
the
album
the
band
largely
eschewed
sampling
in
favour
of
more
synth
experimentation.<sup
id="cite_ref-shunteditorials1_40-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-shunteditorials1-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup>
While
the
chart
performance
of
the
singles
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Strangelove_(song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Strangelove
(song)">Strangelove</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Never_Let_Me_Down_Again?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Never
Let
Me
Down
Again">Never
Let
Me
Down
Again</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Behind_the_Wheel?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Behind
the
Wheel">Behind
the
Wheel</a>&quot;
proved
to
be
disappointing
in
the
UK,
they
performed
well
in
countries
such
as
Canada,
Brazil,
West
Germany,
South
Africa,
Sweden
and
Switzerland,
often
reaching
the
top
10.
<i><a
href="/wiki/Record_Mirror?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Record
Mirror">Record
Mirror</a></i>
described
<i>Music
for
the
Masses</i>
as
&quot;the
most
accomplished
and
sexy
Mode
album
to
date&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-41"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
it
made
a
breakthrough
in
the
American
market,
something
which
the
band
had
failed
to
achieve
with
their
previous
albums.</p>








<p>The
<a
href="/wiki/Music_for_the_Masses_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Music
for
the
Masses
Tour">Music
for
the
Masses
Tour</a>
followed
the
release
of
the
album.
On
7
March
1988
they
played
an
unofficial
gig
(as
it
was
not
officially
announced
that
Depeche
Mode
were
the
band
performing
that
night)
in
the
<a
href="/wiki/Werner-Seelenbinder-Halle?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Werner-Seelenbinder-Halle">Werner-Seelenbinder-Halle</a>,
East
Berlin.<sup
id="cite_ref-42"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup>
At
that
time
the
communist
regime
were
still
in
power
and
Depeche
Mode
were
among
the
very
few
western
bands
that
ever
played
in
the
former
<a
href="/wiki/East_Germany?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="East
Germany">GDR</a>.
Around
the
same
period,
they
also
gave
concerts
in
Budapest
and
Prague
(1988)<sup
id="cite_ref-czechfanzine_43-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-czechfanzine-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup>
in
the
then
still
communist
Hungary
and
Czechoslovakia
respectively.</p>








<p>The
world
tour
ended
on
18
June
1988
with
a
concert
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/Pasadena,_California?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Pasadena,
California">Pasadena</a>
<a
href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_(stadium)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rose
Bowl
(stadium)">Rose
Bowl</a>
with
paid
attendance
of
60,453<sup
id="cite_ref-44"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup>
(the
highest
in
eight
years
for
the
venue).
The
tour
was
a
breakthrough
for
the
band
and
a
massive
success
in
the
United
States.
It
was
documented
in
<i><a
href="/wiki/101_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="101
(album)">101</a></i>
–
a
concert
film
by
<a
href="/wiki/D._A._Pennebaker?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="D.
A.
Pennebaker">D.
A.
Pennebaker</a>
and
its
accompanying
soundtrack
album.
The
film
is
notable
for
its
portrayal
of
fan
interaction.<sup
id="cite_ref-45"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-46"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup>
Alan
Wilder
is
credited
with
coming
up
with
the
name;
the
performance
was
the
101st
and
final
performance
of
the
tour.<sup
id="cite_ref-47"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup>
On
7
September
1988,
Depeche
Mode
performed
&quot;Strangelove&quot;
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/1988_MTV_Video_Music_Awards?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="1988
MTV
Video
Music
Awards">1988
MTV
Video
Music
Awards</a>
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/Universal_Amphitheatre?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Universal
Amphitheatre"
class="mw-redirect">Universal
Amphitheatre</a>
in
Los
Angeles.<sup
id="cite_ref-48"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Violator_and_worldwide_fame_.281989.E2.80.931991.29"><i>Violator</i>
and
worldwide
fame
(1989–1991)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8"
title="Edit
section:
Violator
and
worldwide
fame
(1989–1991)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>In
mid-1989,
the
band
began
recording
in
<a
href="/wiki/Milan?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Milan">Milan</a>
with
producer
<a
href="/wiki/Flood_(producer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Flood
(producer)">Flood</a>
and
engineer
<a
href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Kevorkian?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Fran&ccedil;ois
Kevorkian">Fran&ccedil;ois
Kevorkian</a>.
The
initial
result
of
this
session
was
the
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Personal_Jesus?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Personal
Jesus">Personal
Jesus</a>&quot;.
Prior
to
its
release,
a
marketing
campaign
was
launched
with
advertisements
placed
in
the
personal
columns
of
UK
regional
newspapers
with
the
words
&quot;Your
own
personal
Jesus.&quot;
Later,
the
ads
included
a
phone
number
one
could
dial
to
hear
the
song.
The
resulting
furore
helped
propel
the
single
to
number
13
on
the
UK
charts,
becoming
one
of
their
biggest
sellers
to
date;
in
the
US,
it
was
their
first
gold
single
and
their
first
Top
40
hit
since
&quot;People
Are
People&quot;,
eventually
becoming
the
biggest-selling
12-inch
single
in
<a
href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Warner
Bros.
Records">Warner
Bros.
Records</a>'
history
up
to
that
point.<sup
id="cite_ref-49"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<div
class="quotebox"
style="float:
left;
clear:
left;
margin:
0.5em
1.4em
0.8em
0;
width:
25%;
padding:
6px;
border:
1px
solid
#aaa;
font-size:
88%;
background-color:
#F9F9F9;
padding:10px;">









<div
style="position:
relative;
text-align:
left;">










<div>











<p>&quot;I
think
in
a
way
we've
been
at
the
forefront
of
new
music;
sort
of
chipping
away
at
the
standard
rock
format
stations.&quot;</p>










</div>









</div>









<div
style="text-align:
left;">









Martin
Gore,
stated
to










<i><a
href="/wiki/NME?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="NME">NME</a></i>
–
July
1990.









<sup
id="cite_ref-50"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup>








</div>








</div>








<p>Released
in
January
1990,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Enjoy_the_Silence?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Enjoy
the
Silence">Enjoy
the
Silence</a>&quot;
became
one
of
Depeche
Mode's
most
successful
singles
to
date,
reaching
number
six
in
the
UK
(the
first
Top
10
hit
in
that
country
since
&quot;Master
And
Servant&quot;).
A
few
months
later
it
became
Depeche
Mode's
biggest
hit
in
the
US,
reaching
number
eight
and
earning
the
band
a
second
gold
single.
It
won
'Best
British
single'
at
the
1991
<a
href="/wiki/Brit_Awards?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brit
Awards">Brit
Awards</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-51"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup>
To
promote
their
new
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Violator_(album)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Violator
(album)">Violator</a></i>,
the
band
held
an
in-store
autograph
signing
at
Wherehouse
Entertainment
in
Los
Angeles.
The
event
attracted
approximately
20,000
fans
and
turned
into
a
near
riot.
Some
of
those
who
attended
were
injured
by
being
pressed
against
the
store's
glass
by
the
crowd.<sup
id="cite_ref-52"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup>
As
an
apology
to
the
fans
who
were
injured,
the
band
released
a
limited
edition
cassette
tape
to
fans
living
in
Los
Angeles,
which
was
distributed
through
radio
station
<a
href="/wiki/KROQ-FM?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="KROQ-FM">KROQ</a>
(the
sponsor
of
the
Wherehouse
event).
<i>Violator</i>
went
on
to
reach
Top
10
in
the
UK
and
US.
<i>Violator</i>
was
the
first
of
the
band's
albums
to
enter
the
Top
10
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_200?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Billboard
200">Billboard
200</a>—
reaching
No.
7
and
staying
74
weeks
in
the
chart.
It
has
also
been
<a
href="/wiki/RIAA_certification?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="RIAA
certification">certified</a>
triple
platinum
in
America,
selling
over
4.5
million
units
there.
It
remains
the
band's
best
selling
album
worldwide.<sup
id="cite_ref-53"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup>
Two
more
singles
from
the
album
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Policy_of_Truth?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Policy
of
Truth">Policy
of
Truth</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/World_in_My_Eyes?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="World
in
My
Eyes">World
in
My
Eyes</a>&quot;
were
hits
in
the
UK
with
the
former
also
charting
in
the
US.</p>








<div
class="quotebox"
style="float:
right;
clear:
right;
margin:
0.5em
0
0.8em
1.4em;
width:
25%;
padding:
6px;
border:
1px
solid
#aaa;
font-size:
88%;
background-color:
#F9F9F9;
padding:10px;">









<div
style="position:
relative;
text-align:
left;">










<div>











<p>&quot;I
remember
going
to
see
them
in
Giants
Stadium,
and
they
broke
the
merchandising
record;
of
<a
href="/wiki/Bon_Jovi?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bon
Jovi">Bon
Jovi</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/U2?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="U2">U2</a>
—
all
these
bands
—
Depeche
Mode
were
the
biggest!.&quot;</p>










</div>









</div>









<div
style="text-align:
left;">









Flood,
on










<i><a
href="/wiki/Giants_Stadium?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Giants
Stadium">Giants
Stadium</a></i>
concert.









<sup
id="cite_ref-flood_54-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-flood-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup>








</div>








</div>








<p>The
<a
href="/wiki/World_Violation_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="World
Violation
Tour">World
Violation
Tour</a>
marked
another
high
point
in
Depeche
Mode's
popularity
and
saw
the
band
play
several
stadium
shows
in
the
US.
42,000
tickets
were
sold
within
four
hours
for
a
show
at
Giants
Stadium
and
48,000
tickets
were
sold
within
half-an-hour
of
going
on
sale
for
a
show
at
<a
href="/wiki/Dodger_Stadium?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dodger
Stadium">Dodger
Stadium</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-55"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup>
It
was
estimated
that
1.2
million
fans
saw
this
tour
worldwide.<sup
id="cite_ref-P93_6-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-P93-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
1991,
Depeche
Mode
contribution,
&quot;Death's
Door&quot;
was
released
on
the
accompanying
Warner
Brothers
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Until_the_End_of_the_World_(soundtrack)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Until
the
End
of
the
World
(soundtrack)">Until
the
End
of
the
World:
original
motion
picture
soundtrack</a></i>
for
the
film
<i><a
href="/wiki/Until_the_End_of_the_World?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Until
the
End
of
the
World">Until
the
End
of
the
World</a></i>.
Musical
artists
were
challenged
by
film
director
Wim
Wenders
to
write
music
the
way
they
imagined
they
would
in
the
year
2000,
the
setting
of
the
movie.</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Songs_of_Faith_and_Devotion_and_Wilder.27s_departure_.281992.E2.80.931996.29"><i>Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion</i>
and
Wilder's
departure
(1992–1996)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=Depeche_Mode&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9"
title="Edit
section:
Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion
and
Wilder's
departure
(1992–1996)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>The
members
of
Depeche
Mode
regrouped
in
<a
href="/wiki/Madrid?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Madrid">Madrid</a>
in
January
1992,
Dave
Gahan
had
become
interested
in
the
new
<a
href="/wiki/Grunge?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Grunge">grunge</a>
scene
sweeping
the
U.S.
and
was
influenced
by
the
likes
of
<a
href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Addiction?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jane's
Addiction">Jane's
Addiction</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Soundgarden?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Soundgarden">Soundgarden</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Nirvana_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Nirvana
(band)">Nirvana</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-56"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-contactm1_57-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-contactm1-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<div
class="quotebox"
style="float:
left;
clear:
left;
margin:
0.5em
1.4em
0.8em
0;
width:
30%;
padding:
6px;
border:
1px
solid
#aaa;
font-size:
88%;
background-color:
#F9F9F9;
padding:10px;">









<div
style="position:
relative;
text-align:
left;">










<div>











<p>&quot;There's
so
many
sounds
that
are
created
from
the
voice
that
you
wouldn't
know
were
taken
from
the
voice,
like
rhythm
sounds.
The
number
of
times
I've
been
sitting
in
the
studio
and
said,
'I
wish
I
could
get
a
bass
that
would
just
go
[mimics
wet,
thick
hip-hop
bass-drum
sound].'
Then
I
think,
'Why
can't
I
just
go
[repeats
noise]
into
a
mic
and
sample
it?'
It's
<i>obvious</i>;
you
spend
all
day
trying
to
get
a
synthesizer
to
try
and
create
this
sound
but
you
can
just
go
[repeats
noise]
and
you've
got
it.
Then
you
can
send
it
through
some
other
device
after
that,
and
you've
got
something
that
sounds
absolutely
nothing
like
a
voice,
but
the
source
was
a
voice.
...
It
is
a
very
interesting
process.&quot;</p>










</div>









</div>









<div
style="text-align:
left;">









Alan
Wilder
on
the
genesis
of
some
of
the
sounds
on










<i>Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion</i>,
stated
to










<i>Pulse!
magazine</i>
–
May
1993.









<sup
id="cite_ref-P93_6-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-P93-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup>








</div>








</div>








<p>In
1993
<i><a
href="/wiki/Songs_of_Faith_and_Devotion?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion">Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion</a></i>
again
with
producer
<a
href="/wiki/Flood_(producer)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Flood
(producer)">Flood</a>,
saw
them
experimenting
with
more
organic
arrangements,
based
as
much
on
heavily
distorted
electric
guitars
and
live
drums
(played
by
Alan
Wilder,
whose
debut
as
a
studio
drummer
had
come
on
the
<i>Violator</i>
track
&quot;Clean&quot;)
as
synthesisers.<sup
id="cite_ref-58"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup>
Live
<a
href="/wiki/String_instrument?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="String
instrument">strings</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Uilleann_pipes?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Uilleann
pipes">uilleann
pipes</a>
and
female
<a
href="/wiki/Gospel_music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gospel
music">gospel</a>
vocals
were
other
new
additions
to
the
band's
sound.
The
album
debuted
at
number
one
in
both
the
UK
and
the
US,
only
the
sixth
British
act
to
achieve
such
a
distinction
to
date.<sup
id="cite_ref-flood_54-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-flood-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
first
single
from
the
album
was
the
grunge-influenced
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/I_Feel_You?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="I
Feel
You">I
Feel
You</a>&quot;.
The
gospel
influences
are
most
noticeable
on
the
album's
third
single,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Condemnation_(song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Condemnation
(song)">Condemnation</a>&quot;.
A
symptom
of
the
slow
fracturing
of
the
band,
interviews
given
by
the
band
during
this
period
tended
to
be
conducted
separately,
unlike
earlier
albums,
where
the
band
was
interviewed
as
a
group.<sup
id="cite_ref-P93_6-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-P93-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>The
<a
href="/wiki/Devotional_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Devotional
Tour">Devotional</a>
world
tour
followed.
It
was
documented
by
a
concert
film
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Devotional_(video)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Devotional
(video)">same
name</a>.
The
film
was
directed
by
<a
href="/wiki/Anton_Corbijn?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Anton
Corbijn">Anton
Corbijn</a>
and
in
1995
earned
the
band
their
first
<a
href="/wiki/Grammy_Awards?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Grammy
Awards"
class="mw-redirect">Grammy</a>
nomination.<sup
id="cite_ref-59"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
band's
second
live
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Songs_of_Faith_and_Devotion_Live?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion
Live">Songs
of
Faith
and
Devotion
Live</a></i>,
was
released
in
December
1993.</p>








<p>The
tour
continued
into
1994
with
the
<a
href="/wiki/Exotic_Tour?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Exotic
Tour"
class="mw-redirect">Exotic
Tour</a>,
which
began
in
February
1994
in
South
Africa
and
ended
in
April
in
Mexico.
The
final
leg
of
the
tour,
consisting
of
more
North
American
dates,
followed
shortly
thereafter
and
ran
until
July.
As
a
whole,
the
Devotional
Tour
is
to
date
the
longest
and
most
geographically
diverse
Depeche
Mode
tour,
spanning
fourteen
months
and
159
individual
performances.
<i>Q</i>
magazine
listed
1993
Devotional
Tour
as
&quot;The
Most
Debauched
Rock'n'Roll
Tour
Ever&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-60"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Dave
Gahan's
<a
href="/wiki/Heroin_addiction?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Heroin
addiction"
class="mw-redirect">heroin
addiction</a>
was
starting
to
affect
his
behaviour,
causing
him
to
become
more
erratic
and
introverted.
Martin
Gore
experienced
seizures
and
Andy
Fletcher
declined
to
participate
in
the
second
half
of
the
Exotic
Tour
due
to
&quot;mental
instability&quot;.
During
that
period,
he
was
replaced
on-stage
by
<a
href="/wiki/Daryl_Bamonte?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Daryl
Bamonte">Daryl
Bamonte</a>,
who
had
worked
with
the
band
as
a
personal
assistant
for
many
years.<sup
id="cite_ref-61"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>In
June
1995,
Alan
Wilder
announced
that
he
was
leaving
Depeche
Mode,
explaining,</p>








<blockquote
class="templatequote">









<p>Since
joining
in
1982,
I
have
continually
striven
to
give
total
energy,
enthusiasm
and
commitment
to
the
furthering
of
the
group's
success
and
in
spite
of
a
consistent
imbalance
in
the
distribution
of
the
workload,
willingly
offered
this.
Unfortunately,
within
the
group,
this
level
of
input
never
received
the
respect
and
acknowledgement
that
it
warrants.<sup
id="cite_ref-62"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








</blockquote>








<p>He
continued
to
work
on
his
personal
project
<a
href="/wiki/Recoil_(band)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Recoil
(band)">Recoil</a>,
releasing
a
fourth
album
(<i><a
href="/wiki/Unsound_Methods?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Unsound
Methods">Unsound
Methods</a></i>)
in
1997.
Following
Wilder's
departure,
many
were
sceptical
of
whether
Depeche
Mode
would
ever
record
again.
Gahan's
mental
state
and
drug
habit
became
a
major
source
of
concern,
with
a
near-fatal
<a
href="/wiki/Drug_overdose?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Drug
overdose">overdose</a>
at
a
hotel
in
Los
Angeles.</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Ultra_.281997.E2.80.932000.29"><i>Ultra</i>
(1997–2000)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
